mechanism</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Vse2%2Fppy+Heterointerface&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_VSe2%2FPPy%20heterointerface">VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy heterointerface</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="NLM_p first">As an energy storage system, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been under extensive evaluation for application in energy storage devices. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> Unfortunately, the shortage of lithium resources and its ever-increasing cost prevent the sustainable application of LIBs in the future. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 5 to 8">(5−8)</a> Alternative battery technologies that adopt resource-rich elements and enable high energy density and high safety are therefore extremely desirable. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> Recently, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) and potassium-ion batteries (PIBs) have captured more attention as competitive replacements mainly due to their high abundance, lower prices, and analogous properties to LIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a> Nevertheless, for SIBs and PIBs, the slow reaction kinetics and inevitable volume variation caused by the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> or K<sup>+</sup> severely limit their practical application. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14−17)</a> Hence, it is crucial to design and construct appropriate electrodes capable of delivering ions efficiently and durably.</div><div class="NLM_p">To date, numerous compounds have been extensively investigated for SIB or PIB anode materials, such as carbons, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> alloys, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> metal oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> and layered transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> Particularly, TMDs, generally with a formula of MX<sub>2</sub> (M = Ti, V, Mo, W, <i>etc.</i>; X = S, Se, Te), have generated strong interest owing to their graphite-like layered structure of adjacent layers aggregated by weak van der Waals interactions, which could allow the insertion of guest alkali metal ions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 25 to 29">(25−29)</a> Among them, metallic vanadium diselenide (VSe<sub>2</sub>) has excellent intercalation activity because of its large interlayer distance (6.11 Å), high conductivity, versatile electronic properties, and structural instability caused by particular charge density waves. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 30 to 33">(30−33)</a> In addition, the potential of multielectron transfer of VSe<sub>2</sub> assists the realization of high theoretical capacity and high energy density. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> All these advantages make VSe<sub>2</sub> stand out among many candidates for electrode materials in sodium- and potassium-ion batteries. However, VSe<sub>2</sub> also faces the challenge of severe volume change during charge/discharge cycles, resulting in electrode pulverization and limited cycling performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> To address this issue, the coupling of VSe<sub>2</sub> with carbon-based materials is regarded as a viable strategy to enhance electrochemical properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> For instance, Ming <i>et</i><i>al</i>. prepared carbon-coated ultrathin VSe<sub>2</sub> nanosheet assemblies by simply adding 0.1 M glucose into the reaction solution, and the resultant composite achieved a high specific capacity of 571.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Notably, most of the carbon coating steps were carried out by the thermal decomposition of carbon-based precursors; nevertheless, structural collapse to some extent and grain coarsening usually occurred during high-temperature heating. Therefore, a simple and effective surface coating method without excessive high-temperature processing is desired. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> Conductive polymers, which can not only strengthen the conductivity and possess the advantages of good environmental stability and convenient synthesis but also restrain the volume change, are considered ideal electrode additives. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref39 ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 36, 39 and 40">(36,39,40)</a> Taking these features into consideration, the polymerization of pyrrole to build a structural restraining layer to coat VSe<sub>2</sub> is a natural choice.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this study, the preparation of PPy-encapsulated VSe<sub>2</sub> (denoted as VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy) nanoplates by a simple selenization of the VOOH hollow nanospheres precursor and subsequent <i>in situ</i> PPy coating process is reported. Relying on the inherently superior conductivity in VSe<sub>2</sub>, the conductive and elastic PPy coating layer could maintain structural stability and further increase the charge transfer rate, leading to enhanced rate and cycling performance when serving as an SIB and PIB electrode material. More significantly, the function of PPy in the composite, the mechanism of sodium storage, the detailed ion intercalation process, and the resultant intermediates of such PPy-encapsulated VSe<sub>2</sub> nanoplates were comprehensively explored through non-operando material characterizations, operando electrochemical technologies, and the relevant density functional theory calculations.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As the schematic synthesis illustration in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a shows, the VOOH hollow nanosphere material was prepared <i>via</i> a facile hydrothermal approach and employed as the precursor (the SEM images are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), and the VSe<sub>2</sub> nanoplates were obtained by a high-temperature reaction following the selenizing process. Subsequently, the introduced pyrrole monomers successfully self-assembled to generate a conductive PPy shell on the surface of the VSe<sub>2</sub> nanoplates after <i>in situ</i> chemical oxidation polymerization. The morphologies of VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy captured by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b and c, respectively, revealing that VSe<sub>2</sub> presents as arrays of overlapped nanoplates. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c, the PPy coating layer can be observed to be intimately and uniformly wrapped around the VSe<sub>2</sub> nanoplates with a wrinkled morphology, which is believed to alleviate the agglomeration of VSe<sub>2</sub> nanoplates.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Schematic illustrating the synthetic route of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy and crystal structure of VSe<sub>2</sub>, (b) SEM image of VSe<sub>2</sub>, (c) SEM and (d) TEM images of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, (e) HRTEM image, (f) HAADF-STEM image, and (g) the element mapping images of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, (h) XRD patterns and (i) Raman spectra of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy and bare VSe<sub>2</sub>, and XPS spectra of (j) V 2p and (k) C 1s for VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c00169&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The microstructural details of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy were observed <i>via</i> transmission electron microscopy (TEM), demonstrating the lamellar morphology of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy with a lateral size of several micrometers, as displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of the edge area of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy nanoplates reveals that the lattice fringe, determined to be 0.262 nm, corresponds to the (011) plane of VSe<sub>2</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e). The thickness of the PPy coating layer is 6–9 nm. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f and g, the EDX elemental mappings unambiguously prove the presence and the uniform distributions of the V, Se, C, and N species in VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy. The EDS spectra of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy composite are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. The characteristic X-ray powder diffraction (XRD) peaks of VOOH, shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>, are consistent with the orthorhombic phase FeOOH (JCPDS card no. 74-1877). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>h displays the XRD patterns of the bare VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy. The diffraction peaks of both samples are sharp and match well with hexagonal VSe<sub>2</sub> with a space group of <i>P</i>3<i>m</i>1 (JCPDS card no. 89-1641), indicating the high purity and crystallinity of the VSe<sub>2</sub>. The absence of the characteristic peak for PPy could be ascribed to its amorphous nature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> The content of PPy in VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy was measured to be 16.2 wt % by elemental analysis, which is close to the value of 16.7% determined by thermogravimetric analysis (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). The Raman spectra of VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>i. The peaks at 1391.7 and 1571.1 cm<sup>–1</sup> correspond to the pyrrole ring and C═C bonds, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43" aria-label="reference 42 and 43">(42,43)</a> The evidence of the existence of PPy in the composite is demonstrated in the Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a> (the enlargement of <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5a</a>) provides the FTIR spectra ranging from 500 to 2500 cm<sup>–1</sup> to distinguish VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy more clearly. The FTIR spectrum of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy shows the characteristic absorption peaks at 925, 1205 (assigned to the state of doping in PPy), 1046 (C–H), 1383 (C–N), and 1564 cm<sup>–1</sup> (pyrrole ring), and a detailed description is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a> in the Supporting Information. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 41, 44 and 45">(41,44,45)</a> The valence states of the elements in the as-prepared VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy nanoplates were detected by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The characteristic peaks of all elements in VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, including V, Se, N, O, and C, can be observed in the survey XPS spectrum (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>). For the high-resolution XPS spectra of V 2p (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>j) and Se 3d (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a</a>), the binding energies of the V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks are located at 516.7 and 523.9 eV, respectively, while that of the Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub> characteristic peak is at 55.2 eV. These results indicate that the valence states in the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy nanoplates are V<sup>4+</sup> and Se<sup>2–</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35 ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34 ref35 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 33 to 35, 46 and 47">(33−35,46,47)</a> As shown in the C 1s spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>k, the three fitted peaks located at 284.5, 285.4, and 287.7 eV are related to the C–C, C–N, and C═N<sup>+</sup> bonds, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> In addition, the N 1s spectrum confirms the generation of the PPy coating layer in the final product with fitting peaks at 399.8 (pyrrolic N) and 401.5 eV (C–N<sup>+</sup>), as displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7b</a>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a shows the first three cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> over the range of 0.01–3.0 V. During the initial Na<sup>+</sup> insertion process, sharp cathodic peaks that appeared at 1.54 and 0.4 V are ascribed to the formation of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> and metallic vanadium (with Na<sub>2</sub>Se), respectively. Within the initial desodiation reactions, three marked anodic peaks are found at 1.67, 1.76, and 2.06 V, corresponding to the processes of the recovery of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> and the re-formation of VSe<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref37" aria-label="reference 35 and 37">(35,37)</a> The subsequent scan curves display weakened redox peaks, which might be due to the irreversible decomposition of the electrolyte and generation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a><i>In situ</i> potentio-electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (PEIS) plots were measured during the first cycle at different potentials (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b), namely, open-circuit voltage (OCV), 1.8, 0.2, and 0.01 V (discharging), and 0.5, 1.3, 1.9, and 3.0 V (charging) to provide additional insight into the electrochemical mechanisms and kinetic properties of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The large sum value (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub> + <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of 34.58 Ω composed of the SEI film resistance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>) and the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) in the OCV state decreases to 24.41 Ω in the fully sodiated state (0.01 V), which can be attributed to an activation process as a result of the increased infiltration of electrolyte into the electrode material. The desodiation process (0.5–3.0 V) reflects a small improvement in conductivity (with an <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> + <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value change from 22.04 Ω to 20.73 Ω), which is due to the gradual decrease in the insulating Na<sub>2</sub>Se species within the reverse conversion reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b indicates that the fitting resistance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub> + <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) under each voltage shows a gradual decreasing trend in the whole reaction.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) CV curves of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, (b) <i>in situ</i> PEIS plots at various states of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy in the first cycle and inset depicts the fitted <i>R</i><sub>f</sub>+<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values, (c) <i>in situ</i> XRD patterns contour map of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy collected during the first cycle with the corresponding charge/discharge profiles, (d) <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns at selected voltages and (e) the magnification patterns from the pink box, (f) <i>ex situ</i> HRTEM images after being discharged to 0.01 V and charged to 3.0 V, (g) schematic illustration of the intercalation-conversion mechanism of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy during electrochemical reactions.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c00169&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The structural transition and phase evolution of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy upon sodium insertion and extraction were elucidated by <i>in</i>/<i>ex situ</i> XRD and <i>ex situ</i> HRTEM characterizations. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c displays the <i>in situ</i> XRD patterns measured from 54° to 63°, and the corresponding galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles are depicted on the left, where the color change from black to orange implies an increase in intensity. Upon the insertion of the sodium ions, the intensities of the peaks at 54.6°, 55.0°, and 60.6° of the VSe<sub>2</sub> phase gradually weakened and faded. Meanwhile, new peaks at 57.6° and 62.1° can be observed, corresponding to the (202) plane of both the NaVSe<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub>0.6</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> phases, respectively. The peaks of NaVSe<sub>2</sub> at 57.6° displayed a slight shift to a lower angle upon discharge, indicating an enlarged crystal lattice by Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into the interlayers. In the subsequent charging process, the peak moved back to a higher angle, indicating the existence of the NaVSe<sub>2</sub> phase after the initial cycle. The partial preservation of the NaVSe<sub>2</sub> phase implies that the electrochemical reaction was irreversible to a certain extent. Upon charging, no peak indexed to the Na<sub>0.6</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> phase can be observed, which can be attributed to the small grain size.</div><div class="NLM_p"><i>Ex situ</i> XRD patterns supplement the specific information on the phase evolution. Similarly, after the signal of the peak at 44.5° ((003) plane of the VSe<sub>2</sub> phase) disappeared, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e, the peak at 44.9° indexed to the (018) plane of the NaVSe<sub>2</sub> phase emerged, which underwent a slight shift to a low angle <i>via</i> the discharging process and to a high angle during the charging process. This phenomenon is consistent with the <i>in situ</i> results. At a discharge state of 1.0 V (D1.0 V), as shown <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d, the observed peak at 32.3° represents the signal of VSe, confirming the proceeding of the conversion reaction. At the fully discharged state (0.01 V), the peaks appearing at 37.3° and 47.9° shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d indicate the generation of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and metallic V, respectively. Notably, the peak indexed to the Na<sub>0.6</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> phase appeared at the discharged state of 1.85 V (D1.85 V), and the peak was maintained during the sodium-ion intercalation/deintercalation processes, which is similar to the irreversible phase of the NaVSe<sub>2</sub> phase detected in the <i>in situ</i> XRD contour map. In the initial process of desodiation, the peaks corresponding to the Na<sub>2</sub>Se and V phases gradually faded, accompanied by the final reappearance of VSe<sub>2</sub>. Further evidence was supplied by inspection of the HRTEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f) with the corresponding mapping images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>) of the products at cutoff voltages of 0.01 and 3.0 V, proving the emergence of Na<sub>2</sub>Se and metallic V at the 0.01 V state and the recurrence of VSe<sub>2</sub> at the 3.0 V state. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>, XPS measurements were conducted to investigate the valence evolution of vanadium during cycling. At the discharge state to 1.0 V, the peak of V<sup>3+</sup> at 522.5 eV can be deconvoluted, suggesting the reduction of VSe<sub>2</sub>. When the discharge process was completed, both the peaks of V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and V<sup>3+</sup> shifted toward the lower binding energy. During the charging process, these two peaks in turn moved to higher binding energies, corresponding to the oxidation reaction. It should be noted that the V<sup>3+</sup> peak still existed in the fully charged state, suggesting that it was a partially irreversible reaction. Based on the above analyses, the intercalation–conversion mechanism of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy during the process of sodiation/desodiation is schematically illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>g. The phase transition processes follow the conversion reactions of VSe<sub>2</sub> + 4Na<sup>+</sup> + 4<i>e</i><sup>–</sup> ↔ V + 2Na<sub>2</sub>Se, with the intermediates of Na<sub>0.6</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub>, NaVSe<sub>2</sub>, and VSe, notably, partial Na<sub>0.6</sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> and NaVSe<sub>2</sub> being irreversible.</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performances of the as-obtained VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy and bare VSe<sub>2</sub> were explored as the anode for SIBs. The selected GCD profiles of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy from the first to 200th cycles are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, and the plateaus are well matched with the CV results. The VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy delivered an initial discharge/charge capacity of 471.6/394.2 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, giving a Coulombic efficiency of 83.59%. The cycling performance of VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b. The reversible capacities of the VSe<sub>2</sub> electrode are visibly higher than those of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy in the first five cycles, but the capacity retention declines rapidly. After 20 cycles, the specific capacity of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode increases continuously due to an electroactivation process and reaches a value of 388.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> at the 500th cycle. By comparing the cyclic stability of the bare VSe<sub>2</sub>, VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, and PPy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>) at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, it is concluded that the PPy shells play a pivotal part in maintaining the structural stability (as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> of the SEM images after cycling) but a minor part in the contribution to the total capacity. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c displays the rate capability in comparison of two samples, where the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy anode exhibits good rate performance with capacities of 333.6, 270.9, 266.8, 262.9, and 260.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1, 2, 3, 5, and 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The specific capacity can quickly return to 306.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after intensive high-rate testing, owing to the high conductivity originating from the distinct metallic feature of VSe<sub>2</sub> and the effectively conductive PPy coating. In particular, according to the (dis)charge curves of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy anode at different current densities (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>), the almost overlapping profiles suggest the high nanostructural integrity and favorable stability of the electrochemically active interfaces. The superior rate performance of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy verifies that by supporting the conductivity of VSe<sub>2</sub>, the PPy coating can greatly facilitate electron diffusion and reaction kinetics.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical properties of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy in SIB half-cells: (a) GCD profiles at different cycles, (b) cycling performance at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and (c) rate performance compared with VSe<sub>2</sub>, (d) differential capacity <i>vs</i> potential curves at the selective cycles, and (e) long-term cycling performance at 4 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Electrochemical performances of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy∥Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full-cells: (f) GCD profiles at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, (g) cycling performance at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and (h) rate capability.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c00169&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The related differential charge capacity plots are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d. Three peaks related to the plateaus in the GCD profiles are located at 1.66, 1.76, and 1.98 V in the first charging, corresponding to the multistage deintercalation process of ions, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> which gradually shifted to a lower potential until the 20th cycle, reflecting a diminution in polarization. With increasing cycle number, the peaks at 1.66 and 1.76 V became weaker and then stronger with the plot of the 20th cycle as the borderline. The peak at 1.98 V almost completely disappears, indicating that the transformation feasibility to VSe<sub>2</sub> here declined dramatically and merely affects the electrochemical reaction, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> which is in accord with the <i>in</i>/<i>ex situ</i> characterizations. The long-term cycling durability of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy was estimated at a high current density of 4 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e. Impressively, the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode maintained a high reversible capacity of 324.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 2800 cycles, which further corroborates the good cyclic stability. As supplements, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S13 and S14</a> show the good long-term cyclic stability of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy at 2 and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Furthermore, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a> displays the poor electrochemical properties of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy in the ester electrolyte.</div><div class="NLM_p">What is more exciting is the performance characteristics of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy nanoplates in full-cells using Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as the counter electrode to pair with VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy for assembly. A schematic of the full-cell is displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f presents the first and fifth GCD profiles at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and the initial charge/discharge capacity is 385.0/325.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, with a Coulombic efficiency of 84.54%. The inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f is a photograph of two light-emitting diodes lit by a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>∥VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy full-cell. The cycling performance is depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g, showing a maintained capacity of 253.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 300 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h shows the excellent rate capability of the full-cell, where the specific capacity slowly declines with increasing current density. Concretely, the cell delivered an average capacity of 310.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 250.3 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 243.4 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 237.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and 229.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. It is worth noting that the capacity returned to an average value of 282.7 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> when the current density returned to 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The capacity reached 311.5 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at the 100th cycle. Thus, a great application potential could be expected for the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy nanoplates as anode materials for SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">First-principles calculations were performed to elaborately investigate the reaction kinetics and their effects on the sodium storage performance of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy by building the heterojunction model (side view in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17a</a> and bottom view in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S17a</a>). The 2D section of the differential charge density in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a demonstrates the spontaneous carrier transference from PPy to VSe<sub>2</sub> stemming from the inner driving force of the electron gain and electron loss region for equilibrating the Fermi levels. According to the result, the electron transfer will cause a certain number of positive charges to accumulate around PPy with a smaller work function (4.37 eV), and there are corresponding negative charges around VSe<sub>2</sub> with a larger work function (5.00 eV) (Fermi level: <i>E</i><sub>F</sub>(PPy) &gt; <i>E</i><sub>F</sub>(VSe<sub>2</sub>)), thereby generating a built-in electric field (IEF), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c and d show the calculated density of states (DOS) for bare VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy. For the DOS of VSe<sub>2</sub>, VSe<sub>2</sub> shows certain electronic states around the Fermi level (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c), confirming its metallic nature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref34" aria-label="reference 26 and 34">(26,34)</a> In terms of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, PPy incorporation greatly increased the DOS at the Fermi level, implying increased electrical conductivity. The change in the band structure of VSe<sub>2</sub> after PPy coupling was revealed by calculating the partial density of states (PDOS) of the d-band. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e and f show that the d-band centers are −0.89 and −0.65 eV for VSe<sub>2</sub> and VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy, respectively. The obvious upshifting for VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy affords a higher metal–adsorbate interaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>g displays a schematic illustration of the charge transfer characteristic of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy compared with VSe<sub>2</sub>. Obviously, the VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy heterointerface could accommodate many more electrons under the assistance of the formed IEF and thus motivate high-density electron flow, achieving an overall enhancement in electrical conductivity from the inside out for VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy coupled with the metallic nature of VSe<sub>2</sub>. The result can be verified by the tested electrical conductivity <i>via</i> a four-point probe method with a value of 2891.8 S m<sup>–1</sup>, which is far higher than the 702.7 S m<sup>–1</sup> for bare VSe<sub>2</sub>. Furthermore, the intimately interconnected PPy coating layer effectively relieves the volume change to suit the purpose of maintaining the structural integrity, which was validated by the impressive cyclic stability discussed above. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion on the surface of single VSe<sub>2</sub> and the heterojunction interface of VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy were examined, and their migration paths from both the top view and side view are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>h and i, respectively. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>j, the calculated diffusion energy barriers reflect the ease of sodium-ion migration on the surface of VSe<sub>2</sub> as well as between the VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy heterointerface. The lower values for VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy manifest a positive effect of the heterojunction interface on the improvement in diffusion kinetics and thus high-rate performance. These results, including ion and electron migration, theoretically declared the advantages of the coupling of metallic VSe<sub>2</sub> with conductive PPy, which account well for its superior sodium storage performance.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Theoretical analysis of the reaction kinetics for VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy: (a) 2D electron density difference distribution of the heterostructure, in which the red, green, and brown balls represent V, Se, and C, respectively. The red and blue regions illustrate the electron gain and electron loss, respectively. (b) Electrostatic potential, (c) calculated DOS values in comparison with bare VSe<sub>2</sub>, (e) PDOS values of the V 3d orbitals for VSe<sub>2</sub> and (f) VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, (g) illustration of the boosted charge transfer mechanism, top and side view of Na<sup>+</sup> (golden balls) migration pathways (h) on the surface of VSe<sub>2</sub> and (i) on the VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy heterointerface, and (j) Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion energy barriers.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c00169&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The aforementioned electrochemical analyses and theoretical results verify that the modification of PPy is an efficient strategy to boost the electrochemical properties of VSe<sub>2</sub>. To further investigate the kinetics process of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode in SIBs, CV tests at varying rates from 0.2 to 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup> were performed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a). The peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>v</i>) can be correlated with the power-law relationship of <i>i</i> = <i>av</i><sup><i>b</i></sup>, where variable constants <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> can be acquired by fitting the plots of log(<i>v</i>)–log(<i>i</i>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> Typically, a <i>b</i> value of 0.5 indicates an ideal diffusion-controlled process, whereas a <i>b</i> value of 1.0 refers to a capacitive-controlled process. The inset in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a shows that the <i>b</i> values for the marked peaks are 0.83, 0.79, 1.08, and 0.73, suggesting that the sodium-ion storage of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy was the result of the joint contribution of capacitance and diffusion behaviors. Specifically, the proportion of capacitive behavior involved in the ion storage process at the fixed voltage could be quantitatively calculated through the equation <i>i</i> = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v + k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup>, where <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> is assigned to the capacitive fraction, while <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup> represents the diffusion part. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a> Under the rate of 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, 66.6% of the total capacity is identified as capacitive behavior, as shown in the shaded area in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b. The increasing percentages of the capacitive-controlled behavior with the incremental scan rate from 0.2 to 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup> can be seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c. When the scan rate reaches 1.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, the ratio of capacitive contribution of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy attains a value of 67.6%. The capacitive contribution investigation of VSe<sub>2</sub> is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S18</a>, indicating a smaller contribution to sodium storage than that of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Experimental analyses of the reaction kinetics of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy in SIBs: (a) CV curves and inset depict the <i>b</i>-values, (b) contributions of the diffusion and capacitive processes at 0.8 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, (c) capacitive contribution ratios as a function of scan rate, (d) GITT profiles and the <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_m007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_m007.jpeg" id="_i3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button></span> values as a function of the state in (e) discharging and charge processes, (f) EIS plots compared with VSe<sub>2</sub>, (g) EIS plots tested after different cycles, (h) EIS curves obtained at different temperatures, and (i) the fitted <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> values according to the Arrhenius equation.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/large/nn2c00169_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsnano.2c00169&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The sodium-ion diffusion coefficients (<span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i4"></span>) of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy and VSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes were evaluated by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>d shows the GITT curves of the electrodes at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The changes in potential over time correspond to the rate of sodium-ion diffusion in GITT experiments. The <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i5"></span> values can be obtained based on the equation as follows: <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_m003.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></span> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> The calculated <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_m004.gif" alt="" id="_i7"></span> values are plotted as a function of potential, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e, and a similar change trend of the <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_m005.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></span> values can be found in both the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy and VSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes during the discharging and charging processes. Expressly, the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode shows much higher <span class="NLM_inline-formula"><i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer inline-formula-styles" src="/cms/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/asset/images/medium/nn2c00169_m006.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></span> values than VSe<sub>2</sub>, uncovering the faster diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> in VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f displays the Nyquist plots of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy and VSe<sub>2</sub> in their pristine states. These results reveal that VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy has a smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> by comparing the diameter of the semicircle at high frequency with that of bare VSe<sub>2</sub>, indicating an increased electrical conductivity originating from the PPy coating. The Nyquist plots of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy after 1, 20, 100, and 200 cycles are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>g. The fitted results (shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>) were derived from an equivalent circuit (the inset), which is different from that for VSe<sub>2</sub> shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>f. The interfacial resistance of <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> + <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> presents a minimal change within cycling from the first to the 200th cycle, indicating the robustness and high stability of the SEI layer formed on the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode. Furthermore, the smaller <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> + <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> after cycling than <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> in the pristine state could have been caused by an activation process during the reaction, relying on the enriched active sites within the structure, which is beneficial to the interfacial electrochemical reaction, thus accelerating charge diffusion after cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>h, the EIS curves of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy and VSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes at different temperatures exhibit a similar trend of gradually declining <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values as the temperature rises, which is ascribed to the stepped-up electron migration at high temperature. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>i clearly shows that VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy has a lower activation energy <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> (15.08 kJ mol<sup>–1</sup>) than VSe<sub>2</sub> (19.23 kJ mol<sup>–1</sup>), suggesting a higher fraction of activated molecules and thus fast electrochemical reaction kinetics.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The electrochemical potassium-ion storage performance of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy was further investigated within 0.2–2.8 V. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19a</a> shows the first three CV curves of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. During the first reduction process, a pronounced broad peak is observed at 1.76 V, which represents the K<sup>+</sup> intercalating into the lattice layers of VSe<sub>2</sub> to form K<sub><i>x</i></sub>VSe<sub>2</sub>. The cathodic peak at 0.82 V, which vanished in the next two cycles, corresponds to the generation of an SEI layer and irreversible reactions. The following sharp reductive peak over 0.2–0.6 V is ascribed to the conversion reaction of K<sub><i>x</i></sub>VSe<sub>2</sub> to metallic V and K<sub>2</sub>Se. During the first oxidation process, the two peaks at 1.4 and 2.0 V are derived from the multistep phase transformation from K<sub>2</sub>Se to VSe<sub>2</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a><a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19b</a> shows the GCD profiles of selective cycles from the cycling performance of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as depicted in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19c</a>. The VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode achieved a high reversible capacity of 203.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> over 80 cycles. <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19d</a> shows that the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy electrode displayed an impressive rate capability, delivering high average reversible capacities of 184.6, 171.7, 161.7, and 148.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1, 2, 3, and 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. An average capacity of 202.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> was recovered when the current density returned to 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, which is smaller than that cycled in the first round, demonstrating little structural failure after cycling under a high current density. Attracted by the good rate capability, EIS and GITT were employed for testing to investigate the detailed kinetic properties. The EIS plots in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S19e</a> show a decreasing trend in <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> after cycling, suggesting the increased wetting of the electrode by the electrolyte. The K migration paths, including top and side views of the surface of VSe<sub>2</sub> and the heterojunction interface of VSe<sub>2</sub>/PPy, are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20a and b</a>, respectively. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S20c</a>, the diffusion energy barriers of potassium ions within the interface between VSe<sub>2</sub> and PPy are smaller than those in VSe<sub>2</sub>, indicating increased reaction kinetics for VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy in PIBs.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a hybrid material consisting of VSe<sub>2</sub> nanoplates overcoated by PPy sheaths was fabricated <i>via</i> selenization treatment of hollow VOOH nanospheres, whose properties were tested in both SIB and PIB systems. The natural high conductivity of VSe<sub>2</sub> coupled with the bifunctional effects of PPy increased the charge diffusion within and outside and cushioned the volumetric variation, endowing the composite with outstanding cycling durability and rate capability. As a result, when used as the anode for SIBs, VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy showed a high reversible capacity (388.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 500 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>), superior rate capability (260.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>), and excellent cyclability (324.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 2800 cycles at 4 A g<sup>–1</sup>). The VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy sample also proved to have decent potassium storage properties with a high reversible capacity (203.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup>, 148.6 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>). Furthermore, the sodium-ion full-cell pairing of the VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy anode with the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode has been demonstrated to be equipped with good rate and cycling performance. The GITT test results confirmed the enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, which is essential for achieving high-rate capability, and this was further manifested by the reduced ion migration barriers compared with bare VSe<sub>2</sub><i>via</i> theoretical calculations. More significantly, <i>in</i>/<i>ex situ</i> XRD, <i>ex situ</i> TEM, and <i>in situ</i> PEIS characterizations further revealed the insertion and conversion reaction mechanisms of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction. Moreover, DFT calculations reveal that the combined high conductivity and speedy ion diffusion endow VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy with comprehensive high performance as a promising anode material for SIBs and PIBs.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Synthetic Samples</h3><div id="sec4_1_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Fabrication of VOOH Hollow Nanospheres</h4><div class="NLM_p last">VOOH hollow nanospheres (VOOH HNSs) were obtained <i>via</i> a hydrothermal process on the basis of previous literature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> In detail, 0.234 g of NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> was added to 45 mL of deionized water with vigorously stirring for 10 min at room temperature. A HCl (1 M) solution (1 mL) was dispersed into the above solution at a speed of 0.2 mL min<sup>–1</sup> to ensure the transformation to a transparent yellow solution. Subsequently, 3 mL of N<sub>2</sub>H<sub>4</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O was introduced into the above solution with continuous stirring for another 0.5 h. The solution was transferred into a 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave before being heated at 160 °C for 8 h. The obtained precipitate was collected by centrifugation after rinsing.</div></div><div id="sec4_1_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Fabrication of VSe<sub>2</sub> Nanoplates</h4><div class="NLM_p last">First, 1 mmol of the as-prepared VOOH HNSs and 2 mmol of SeO<sub>2</sub> were dissolved in 30 mL of deionized water under stirring for 5 min. Afterward, 6 mL of HCOOH was added dropwise to the above solution and kept vigorously stirred for 15 min. Finally, the mixture was transferred to a 50 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave before being heated at 180 °C for 36 h. Then, the precipitate was collected by centrifugation after rinsing.</div></div><div id="sec4_1_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i15"> Fabrication of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy Nanoplates</h4><div class="NLM_p last">One hundred milligrams of the obtained VSe<sub>2</sub> nanoplates was uniformly dispersed in 40 mL of deionized water containing 5 mg of C<sub>12</sub>H<sub>25</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>Na by sonication for 1 h. Next, 25 μL of pyrrole was dispersed into the above solution and vigorously stirred for 1 h. Then, 5 mL of a (NH<sub>4</sub>)S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>8</sub> (0.1 M) aqueous solution was added dropwise into this solution under stirring. After 4 h, the final product was rinsed and collected.</div></div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i16"> Material Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray diffraction patterns (Rigaku D/max 2000 PC, Japan) and Raman spectra (Jobin-Yvon Lab RAM HR-800) were used to investigate the detailed crystalline structure. The physical–chemical properties were tested by a N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm analyzer (ASAP2420), and an X-ray Photodectron spectroscope (Kratos Analytical Ltd., U.K.). The surface morphologies and internal structures were explored through scanning electron microscopy (JEOL JMS-6700F) and transmission electron microscopy (TEOL JEM-2010F).</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To prepare the working electrodes, 80 wt % active material, 10 wt % Super P, and 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) were uniformly mixed with <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidene (NMP) as a solvent. The formed slurry was blade-coated onto a copper foil and vacuum-dried at 120 °C overnight. The coil cells were assembled by pairing the working electrode with a sodium or potassium foil acting as a counter electrode and a glass fiber filter as the separator. The applied electrolyte for the SIBs was 1 M NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether (DIGLYME), and 1 M KPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in dimethoxyethane (DME) was used as the electrolyte for the PIBs. The average loading mass in each of the electrodes in both the NIBs and PIBs was approximately 0.8 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. For full-cell assemblies, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NPV) was used as the counter electrode in each. The N/P ratio of the electrode was approximately 1.2:1, and the average loading mass of the NPV cathode material was approximately 2.7 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. Galvanostatic charge/discharge and galvanostatic intermittent titration data were acquired by a LAND-CT2011A multichannel battery testing system (Wuhan Lanhe, China). CV and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were recorded on a VSP multichannel electrochemical workstation (Biologic, France). All electrochemical measurements were conducted at 26 °C. The DFT calculations were carried out on the basis of the conditions and methods. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a></div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i18">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">XRD pattern and SEM images of VOOH; EDS spectrum, TGA curves, FTIR spectra, and XPS spectra of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy; FTIR spectra vibration modes of the functional groups in PPy; mechanism investigation involving mapping, <i>ex situ</i> XPS, and the specific reaction equations in the SIBs and PIBs systems; cycling performance of PPy, SEM images of the electrode after cycling, cycling performances of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy, and electrochemical performance of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy using ester electrolyte; schematic illustration of the full-cells; DFT calculation models; equivalent circuit fitting results of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy; and potassium-ion storage performance of VSe<sub>2</sub>@PPy (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsnano.2c00169/suppl_file/nn2c00169_si_001.pdf">nn2c00169_si_001.pdf (1.88 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsnano.2c00169" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                10.1038/s41578-019-0166-4</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D276-294%26journal%3DNat.%2BRev.%2BMater.%26author%3DM.%2BLi%26author%3DJ.%2BLu%26author%3DX.%2BJi%26author%3DY.%2BLi%26author%3DY.%2BShao%26author%3DZ.%2BChen%26author%3DC.%2BZhong%26author%3DK.%2BAmine%26title%3DDesign%2BStrategies%2Bfor%2BNonaqueous%2BMultivalent-Ion%2Band%2BMonovalent-Ion%2BBattery%2BAnodes%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fs41578-019-0166-4&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsnano.2c00169&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fs41578-019-0166-4&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Design strategies for nonaqueous multivalent-ion and monovalent-ion battery anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Matthew; Lu, Jun; Ji, Xiulei; Li, Yanguang; Shao, Yuyan; Chen, Zhongwei; Zhong, Cheng; Amine, Khalil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Reviews Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">276-294</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NRMADL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2058-8437</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The inability of current battery technologies to keep up with the performance requirements of industry is pushing forward developments in electrochem.  Specifically, the batteries neg. electrode, the anode, presents many unique chem., phys. and engineering challenges.  Lithium-based battery technologies have dominated the past decade, but concerns about the limited supply of lithium in the Earths crust have led researchers to look towards alternative metal-ion technologies.  Various alkali metals (such as sodium and potassium) and alkali earth metals (such as magnesium and calcium) have attracted significant research interest.  In this Review, we analyze these technologies in a coherent manner, addressing the problems of each type of anode, rather than those of specific types of metal-ion batteries.  Covering direct metal plating and stripping, intercalation-based, alloy-based and conversion-reaction-based anode technologies, this anal. will offer the reader a comprehensive understanding of the behavior of different metal-ion anodes and of what can be learned by transferring knowledge between these different systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700488"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H. K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Unique Structural Design and Strategies for Germanium-Based Anode Materials Toward Enhanced Lithium Storage</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">General Liquid-Driven Coaxial Flow Focusing Preparation of Novel Microcapsules for Rechargeable Magnesium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lin, Xirong; Liu, Jinyun; Zhang, Haikuo; Zhong, Yan; Zhu, Mengfei; Zhou, Ting; Qiao, Xue; Zhang, Huigang; Han, Tianli; Li, Jinjin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Science (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Magnesium batteries have been considered promising candidates for next-generation energy storage systems owing to their high energy d., good safety without dendrite formation, and low cost of magnesium resources.  However, high-performance cathodes with stable capacity, good cond., and fast ions transport are needed, since many conventional cathodes possess a low performance and poor prepn. controllability.  Herein, a liq.-driven coaxial flow focusing (LDCFF) approach for prepg. a novel microcapsule system with controllable size, high loading, and stable magnesium-storage performance is presented.  Taking the MoS2-infilled microcapsule as a case study, the magnesium battery cathode based on the microcapsules displays a capacity of 100 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles.  High capacity retention is achieved at both low and high temps. of -10, -5, and 45°C, and a stable rate-performance is also obtained.  The influences of the liq. flow rates on the size and shell thickness of the microcapsules are investigated; and electron and ion diffusion properties are also studied by first-principle calcns.  The presented LDCFF method is quite general, and the high performance of the microcapsules enables them to find broad applications for making emerging energy-storage materials and secondary battery systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201701592"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Three-dimensional (3D) graphene materials, consisting of unstacked two-dimensional (2D) graphene sheets serving as building blocks, have been widely reported in recent years.  Various synthetic methods have been employed to prep. graphene materials with diverse 3D architectures, which show potential in a wide range of applications such as energy and environmental technologies.  In particular, application of 3D graphene-based materials in metal (Li, Na, K, Mg, Al)-ion battery (MIB) systems has been reported often in recent years.  However, the crit. roles of 3D graphene materials in MIB systems have not been comprehensively discussed.  Herein, we first summarize the configurations and prepn. of 3D graphene materials.  Second, we illustrate the metal-ion storage mechanisms in graphene layers, i.e. intercalation and adsorption.  Third, we emphasize the superior functionalities of 3D graphene architectures as supporting and encapsulated materials in MIB applications.  Last, we discuss the merits and drawbacks of various 3D graphene architectures in MIB systems.  We aim to present a comprehensive understanding of 3D graphene materials and guide directions for future MIB design.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202004613"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wei, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tian, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qian, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Recent Advances of Emerging 2D MXene for Stable and Dendrite-Free Metal Anodes</span>. <i>Adv.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Recent Advances of Emerging 2D MXene for Stable and Dendrite-Free Metal Anodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wei, Chuanliang; Tao, Yuan; An, Yongling; Tian, Yuan; Zhang, Yuchan; Feng, Jinkui; Qian, Yitai</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Metal anodes based on a plating/stripping electrochem. such as metallic Li, Na, K, Zn, Mg, Ca, Al, and Fe have attracted widespread attention over the past several years because of their high theor. specific capacity, low electrochem. potential, and superior electronic cond.  Metal anodes can be paired with cathodes to construct high-energy-d. rechargeable metal batteries.  However, inherent issues including large vol. changes, uncontrollable growth of dangerous dendrites, and an unstable solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) hinder their further development.  MXene as an emerging 2D material has shown great potential to address the inherent issues of metal anodes due to its 2D structure, abundant surface functional groups, and the ability to construct macroscopic architectures.  To date, under the assistance of MXene, various strategies have been proposed to achieve stable and dendrite-free metal anodes, such as MXene-based host design, designing metalphilic MXene-based substrates, modifying the metal surface with MXene, constructing MXene arrays, and decorating separators or electrolytes with MXene.  Herein the applications and advances of MXene in stable and dendrite-free metal anodes are carefully summarized and analyzed.  Some perspectives and outlooks for future research are also proposed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.202007247"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shin, K.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Multiple Active Sites Carbonaceous Anodes for Na+ Storage: Synthesis, Electrochemical Properties and Reaction Mechanism Analysis</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Yun; Shin, Kang Ho; Yu, Yufeng; Hu, Yezhou; Liang, Jianing; Chen, Ke; Yuan, Haocheng; Park, Ho Seok; Wang, Deli</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">31</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2007247</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Owing to the earth-abundant resources, cost effective materials and stable electrochem. properties, sodium-ions batteries (SIBs) show long-term potential in responding to the rapid consumption of lithium resources and the ever-increasing development of new energy storage devices.  Nevertheless, the intrinsic properties of the large ion radius (Na+ 1.02 Å vs Li+ 0.76 Å) and pos. redn. potential (Na/Na+ -2.71 V vs Li/Li+ -3.04 V) may impede ion diffusion, thus causing serious vol. expansion, resulting in poor cycling stability.  To address these issues, the incorporation of active sites into carbonaceous anode is considered as an efficient strategy to enhance interfacial compatibility, enlarge interlayer distance, and supply reversible Faradic pseudo-capacitance.  Herein, the multiple active sites carbonaceous anodes for SIBs anode are comprehensively reviewed.  Typically, carbonaceous materials are categorized into diffusion and surface controlled based on Na storage mechanism, and the concepts of intrinsic/extrinsic active sites are proposed according to the types of active sites.  Furthermore, to reveal the reaction kinetics and guide the rational design of high performance anodes, the (spectro) electrochem. anal. methods and corresponding key parameters are introduced.  Addnl., primary superiorities, essential issues, and supposed solns. of multiple active sites carbonaceous Na anodes are discussed and the future development directions are also proposed.  This review may provide new design thoughts for high performance carbonaceous Na storage anodes.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2021.128787"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qin, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sb<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>-Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> Microrods with the Combined Action of Carbon Encapsulation and rGO Confinement for Improving High Cycle Stability in Sodium/Potassium Storage</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">For sodium and potassium storage, the inevitably large vol. variation in the process of charge/discharge for the anode materials with conversion and alloying mechanisms leads to unsatisfactory structural stability and capacity retention.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Batteries based on multivalent metals have the potential to meet the future needs of large-scale energy storage, due to the relatively high abundance of elements such as magnesium, calcium, aluminum and zinc in the Earth's crust.  However, the complexity of multivalent metal-ion chemistries has led to rampant confusions, tech. challenges, and eventually doubts and uncertainties about the future of these technologies.  In this Review, we clarify the key strengths as well as common misconceptions of multivalent metal-based batteries.  We then examine the growth behavior of metal anodes, which is crucial for their safety promises but hitherto unestablished.  We further discuss scrutiny of anode efficiency and cathode storage mechanism pertaining to complications arising from electrolyte solns.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational formation of solid electrolyte interface for high-rate potassium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Bo; Yuan, Fei; Li, Wen; Wang, Qiujun; Ma, Xiumei; Gu, Lin; Sun, Huilan; Xi, Kai; Zhang, Di; Wang, Wei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
